Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/medium/nl9b04829_0009.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/large/nl9b04829_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Bismuth (Bi) has been demonstrated as a promising anode for Na-ion batteries (NIBs) because it has high gravimetry (386 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) and volumetric capacity (3800 mA h cm<sup>–3</sup>). However, Bi suffers from large volume expansion during sodiation, leading to poor electrochemical performance. The construction of a nanostructure with sufficient void space to accommodate the volume change has been proven effective for achieving prolonged cycling stability. However the excessive void space will definitely decrease the volumetric energy density of the battery. Herein, we design optimized Bi@Void@C nanospheres (Bi@Void@C-2) with yolk–shell structure that exhibit the best cycling performance and enhanced volumetric energy density. The optimized void space not only could buffer the volume change of the Bi nanosphere but also could keep the high volumetric energy density of the battery. The Bi@Void@C-2 shows an excellent rate capacity of 173 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at ultrahigh current density of 100 A g<sup>–1</sup> and long-cycle life (198 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 20 A g<sup>–1</sup> over 10 000 cycles). The origin of the superior performance is achieved through in-depth fundamental studies during battery operation using <i>in situ</i> X-ray diffraction (XRD) and <i>in situ</i> transmission electron microscope (TEM), complemented by theoretical calculations and <i>ex situ</i> TEM observation. Our rational design provides insights for anode materials with large volume variation, especially for conversion type and alloying type mechanism materials for batteries (i.e., Li-ion batteries, Na-ion batteries).</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D758%26pageCount%3D10%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DHai%2BYang%252C%2BLin-Wei%2BChen%252C%2BFuxiang%2BHe%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D20%26issueNum%3D1%26contentID%3Dacs.nanolett.9b04829%26title%3DOptimizing%2Bthe%2BVoid%2BSize%2Bof%2BYolk%25E2%2580%2593Shell%2BBi%2540Void%2540C%2BNanospheres%2Bfor%2BHigh-Power-Density%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D10%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1530-6984%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dnalefd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D767%26publicationDate%3DJanuary%2B2020" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2019 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Batteries" class="article__tags__link">Batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291338&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrical properties" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrical%20properties" class="article__tags__link">Electrical properties</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291251&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Sodium" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Sodium" class="article__tags__link">Sodium</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292275&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Transmission electron microscopy" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Transmission%20electron%20microscopy" class="article__tags__link">Transmission electron microscopy</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Sodium-ion%20batteries">Sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Bismuth&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_bismuth">bismuth</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Yolk+Shell+Structure&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_yolk%20shell%20structure">yolk shell structure</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=High+Power+Density&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_high%20power%20density">high power density</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Long-life&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_long-life">long-life</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><p class="first">Electrochemical energy storage has driven considerable attention in powering electric vehicles and portable devices because of its high energy and high power densities. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> However, the uneven and limited distribution of lithium resources on earth restricts its development for some practical applications (i.e., large-scale energy storage system). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3 ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 3 to 5">(3−5)</a> Given that the standard reduction potential of sodium is close to that of lithium (−2.71 and −3.04 V vs standard hydrogen electrode, respectively), the sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) are believed likely to possess relatively high energy density. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> On the other hand, sodium is abundant in the ocean, resulting in the cheaper price of raw materials for NIBs than that of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 7 and 8">(7,8)</a></p><p>The performance advancements of NIBs are closely related to the development of electrode materials. Nowadays, a variety of cathodes for NIBs have been reported, including sodium oxides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 9 and 10">(9,10)</a> transition metal fluorides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 11 and 12">(11,12)</a> and sodium phosphates. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 13 and 14">(13,14)</a> However, it is still a great challenge to design suitable anodes for NIBs with a moderate voltage platform, high power density, and long cycling life. First of all, the anode should possess a moderate voltage window between 0.2 and 1.0 V to balance the energy density and safety performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> The hard carbon anodes deliver a low voltage platform (∼0.1 V) but face great problems in sodium dendritic growth. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> On the other hand, the transition metal sulfides/selenides imply high specific capacity with high voltage platforms above 1.0 V, restricting their practical application. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 17 to 21">(17−21)</a> Second, the ability of fast charge/discharge for batteries is a key indicator to evaluate the practicability. To our knowledge, most of the anode materials that have been reported can only charge/discharge at a current density below 20 A g<sup>–1</sup> with limited cycling stability of about 2000 cycles. Therefore, searching for an anode material with a high rate capability is highly desirable yet quite challenging.</p><p>Bismuth, as a promising anode candidate, possesses a gravimetric specific capacity of 386 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and a high volumetric specific capacity of about 3800 mA h cm<sup>–3</sup> based on the alloying reaction mechanism with the formation of Na<sub>3</sub>Bi. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> Besides, the Bi anode exhibits a favorable reaction potential at about 0.5 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), which not only alleviates sodium dendritic growth but also provides a high energy density for practicability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref23" aria-label="reference 15 and 23">(15,23)</a> Notwithstanding, its dramatic volume variation during the alloying/dealloying process reaches up to 352%, leading to pulverization and cracking of the electrodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 24 and 25">(24,25)</a> Designing suitable electrode materials with a rational morphology and incorporating Bi nanoparticles into carbonaceous materials are two main strategies to relieve the volume expansion and improve the interfacial contact area between the electrode and the electrolyte. For example, Wang et al. found that the Bi particles evolved to have porous integrity upon the cycling process, which achieved a high capacity that was close to theoretical capacity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> Xue et al. have constructed bismuth nanorods encapsulated in carbon nanotube composites that maintained 368 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> All of these success designs have demonstrated that well-defined void space offers free space to accommodate the full expansion of active materials without breaking the outer protective shell, leading to improved electrochemical performance. However, excess void space may significantly decrease the volumetric energy density. This means that the void space accommodates the volume expansion, but it sacrifices the volumetric energy density. Thus, it is highly desirable to optimize the void space to realize long-cycle life, high rate capability, and reasonable volumetric energy density. Until now, to the best of our knowledge, there have been few reported successes in optimizing the void space of high-power-density Bi-based anode materials with delicate yolk–shell structures.</p><p>Herein, we designed a series of yolk–shell structures of Bi@carbon nanospheres. The void volume of the yolk–shell structure is adjusted to buffer the volume expansion of the Bi core. The obtained samples with different void sizes were denoted as Bi@Void@C-<i>x</i> (<i>x</i> = 1, 2, 3). The Bi@Void@C-1 shows the smallest void size; the carbon shell bursts after repeated charge/discharge. The Bi@Void@C-3 possesses the excess void space that increases the diffusion length of the sodium ion and reduces the volumetric energy density. The Bi@Void@C-2 with the optimized void space demonstrates a remarkable reversible capacity of about 198 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 20 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 10 000 cycles (51% of the theoretical capacity of Bi) and excellent rate performance of about 170 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 100 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The outstanding sodium storage capacity should be ascribed to the following merits: (1) Optimized void volume could accommodate the serious volume variation, as proven directly by <i>in situ</i> transmission electron microscopy (TEM) observations. (2) The porous carbon layer offers an interconnected conductive pathway for rapid ionic/electronic transport. (3) The tiny Bi nanoparticles can shorten the electronic/ion diffusion length, as elaborated by density functional theory (DFT) theoretical calculation and <i>ab initio</i> molecular dynamics (AIMD) simulation. (4) Another merit is the reversible alloying reactions, as revealed by <i>in situ</i> X-ray diffraction (XRD).</p><p>The facile preparation process of Bi@Void@C is schematically demonstrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a. First, the Bi nanospheres were synthesized via a typical solvothermal process. Subsequently, the SiO<sub>2</sub> and phenolic resin (RF) were coated on the Bi nanospheres to form Bi@SiO<sub>2</sub>@RF with a three-layer construction. The size of the void volume could be easily adjusted by controlling the additive amount of tetraethyl orthosilicate (TEOS). The RF coating layer was transformed into the carbon layer after calcination at inert atmosphere, the as-obtained sample labeled as Bi@SiO<sub>2</sub>@C. Finally, the SiO<sub>2</sub> template was removed by NaOH aqueous solution, leaving the void at the location occupied by SiO<sub>2</sub>. Considering the volume expansion ratio of Bi during sodiation (≈352%), the desirable sacrificial coating SiO<sub>2</sub> layer should have a thickness of 80 nm to enable the Bi core to expand freely. Based on this consideration, the Bi@Void@C-2, which is templated from Bi@SiO<sub>2</sub>@RF-2 with a silica thickness of 100 nm, has been designed as the best one.</p><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/medium/nl9b04829_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/large/nl9b04829_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Schematic illustration of the synthesis process of Bi@Void@C. (b) Schematic illustration of Bi@Void@C with small void size, optimized void size, and excess void size upon cycling.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/large/nl9b04829_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>The schematic diagrams for the sodiation/desodiation process of Bi@Void@C with various void volumes are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b, including limited space of Bi@Void@C-1, optimized space of Bi@Void@C-2, and excess space of Bi@Void@C-3, respectively. The Bi@Void@C-1 with inadequate void space broke after cycles, rendering the capacity as faded away. The Bi@Void@C-1 and Bi@Void@C-2 possess sufficient void space to accommodate the volume expansion, leading to the stable structure of electrodes during cycling. It is worth mentioning that the Bi@Void@C-2 with optimized void space exhibits higher volumetric density than Bi@Void@C-3 because of higher packing density (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/suppl_file/nl9b04829_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>).</p><p><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a–c shows the scanning electron microscope (SEM) images of Bi@Void@RF-1, Bi@Void@RF-2, and Bi@Void@RF-3, respectively. The average diameters of these samples are 200, 310, and 380 nm, respectively, which are bigger than that of pristine Bi nanospheres with an average size of 150 nm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/suppl_file/nl9b04829_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). As displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d–f, the TEM images demonstrate that these samples possess a three-layer structure, consisting of Bi nanospheres, SiO<sub>2</sub>, and RF from inside to outside, respectively. After calcination, the three layered nanospheres are well maintained, and the diameters of the Bi@SiO<sub>2</sub>@C-1, Bi@SiO<sub>2</sub>@C-2, and Bi@SiO<sub>2</sub>@C-3 are decreased a little more than those of the precursors (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>g–i). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>j–l displays the TEM images of as-obtained Bi@Void@C-1, Bi@Void@C-2, and Bi@Void@C-3. The interlayer gap between the inner shell of Bi and outer shell of carbon displays a characteristic yolk–shell structure. The major difference of these samples was the void volume with the ratio of approximately 1:10:20. The thickness of the carbon layer for these samples is about 10–20 nm which is beneficial to the diffusion of sodium ions. It should be noted that the carbon layer thickness of Bi@Void@C-3 is a little thicker than those of Bi@Void@C-2 and Bi@Void@C-1, because the excessively thin carbon shell with a lot of void space will shrivel. The high-resolution transmission electronic microscopy (HRTEM) image of Bi@Void@C-2 in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>m reveals a spacing distance of about 3.28 nm, corresponding to the (012) plane of the Bi nanoparticle. Besides, the carbon shell (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>n) shows an amorphous structure with flourishing porous texture. The high-angle annular dark field (HADDF) scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM) image of Bi@SiO<sub>2</sub>@C-2 and the corresponding energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) elemental mapping of C, N, Bi, and Si elements are exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>o,p, confirming the three-layered structure of Bi@SiO<sub>2</sub>@C-2. After the etching of the SiO<sub>2</sub>, there is no Si in the final product of Bi@Void@C-2 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/suppl_file/nl9b04829_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>).</p><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/medium/nl9b04829_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/large/nl9b04829_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. SEM and TEM images of (a, d) Bi@SiO<sub>2</sub>@RF-1, (b, e) Bi@SiO<sub>2</sub>@RF-2, and (c, f) Bi@SiO<sub>2</sub>@RF-3, respectively. TEM images of (g) Bi@SiO<sub>2</sub>@C-1, (h) Bi@SiO<sub>2</sub>@C-2, and (i) Bi@SiO<sub>2</sub>@C-3, respectively. TEM images of (j) Bi@Void@C-1, (k) Bi@Void@C-2, and (l) Bi@Void@C-3, respectively. (m, n) HRTEM, (o) HADDF-STEM image, and (p) corresponding elemental mapping of Bi@SiO<sub>2</sub>@C-2.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/large/nl9b04829_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>The crystallographic structures of Bi@Void@C-1, Bi@Void@C-2, and Bi@Void@C-3 are confirmed by X-ray diffraction (XRD). As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a, all the peaks in the diffraction patterns can be gratifyingly assigned to a hexagonal phase with <i>R</i>3̅<i>m</i> space group (JCPDS 85-1329). There are no apparent peaks of carbon detected in XRD patterns, indicating the amorphous carbon structure. The Raman spectrum is highly sensitive to characterize the carbon materials. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b, two broad peaks centered at 1350 and 1597 cm<sup>–1</sup> can be indexed to the D band and G band of carbon, corresponding to defective carbon and graphitic sp<sup>2</sup>-type carbon, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 27 and 28">(27,28)</a> The three samples have similar XRD patterns and Raman spectrum. The Bi content of Bi@Void@C-1, Bi@Void@C-2, and Bi@Void@C-3 is about 75, 62, and 56 wt % evaluated by thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/suppl_file/nl9b04829_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). The elemental composition and the chemical environment of Bi@Void@C-2 are investigated by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). The peaks in the high-resolution C 1s spectrum can be divided into three bands around 284.6, 286.0, and 287.7 eV, assigned to C–C, C–N, and C–O bonds, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 29 and 30">(29,30)</a> The high-resolution spectrum of N 1s for Bi@Void@C-2 is exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d. Three N species can be observed: quaternary N (N-Q at 401.4 eV), pyrrolic N (N-5 at 400.7 eV), and pyridinic N (N-6 at 398.3 eV), respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30 ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 30 and 31">(30,31)</a> The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherm curves reveal high Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) specific surface area (79.6, 168.9, and 256.5 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> for Bi@Void@C-1, Bi@Void@C-2, and Bi@Void@C-3, respectively) of these three samples (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e). The pore-size distribution curves calculated by the DFT method imply lots of mesopores at around 10 nm of these samples, which allows fast diffusion of the sodium ion (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f).</p><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/medium/nl9b04829_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/large/nl9b04829_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) XRD patterns and (b) Raman spectroscopy of Bi@Void@C-1, Bi@Void@C-2, and Bi@Void@C-3. (c) C 1s and (d) N 1s spectra of Bi@Void@C-2. (e) N<sub>2</sub> absorption–desorption isotherms and (f) pore distribution of Bi@Void@C-1, Bi@Void@C-2, and Bi@Void@C-3.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/large/nl9b04829_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>The sodium storage behaviors of three yolk–shell samples were tested in sodium-ion half-cells. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a shows the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of Bi@Void@C-2 for the first four cycles at a sweep rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> between 0.01 and 1.5 V. In the first sodiation process, the broad peak between 1.4 and 1.0 V, disappearing in subsequent cycles, can be attributed to irreversible decomposition of electrolyte at the interface of electrode and electrolyte. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> Besides, a dominating cathodic peak is observed at 0.59 V, corresponding to sodium alloying with Bi to produce NaBi. The following cathodic sweep is characterized by a sharp peak centered at 0.41 V with a shoulder peak at 0.37 V, suggesting the reaction between NaBi and Na to the formation of Na<sub>3</sub>Bi. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref33" aria-label="reference 15 and 33">(15,33)</a> The peak at around 0.1 V is assigned to sodium ion inserted into carbon shell. During the first desodiation process, two peaks are visible at around 0.55 and 0.72 V, corresponding to the dealloying process of Na<sub>3</sub>Bi to NaBi and Bi, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref33" aria-label="reference 15 and 33">(15,33)</a> In the subsequent charge/discharge cycles, two pairs of redox peaks are centered at 0.59/0.72 V (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a>) and 0.40/0.55 V (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a>), respectively, which can be summarized below:<span class="NLM_disp-formula"></span></p><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/medium/nl9b04829_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i2"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/medium/nl9b04829_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i3"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span>The difference between the initial and later cycle results from the formation of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer, and the peaks’ slight location shift probably because of the activation of the Bi electrode. The CV curves of the Bi@Void@C-2 are almost overlapped after the first cycle, showing a highly reversible electrochemical reaction.<p></p><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/medium/nl9b04829_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/large/nl9b04829_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Sodium-ion storage behaviors. (a) CV curves and (b) galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of Bi@Void@C-2. (c) Cycling stability at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> and (d) rate performance after the electrochemical activation process of Bi@Void@C-1, Bi@Void@C-2, and Bi@Void@C-3. (e) Long-term cycling stability at 20 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (f) Comparison of the capacity retention, current density, and cycle number between Bi@Void@C-2 with other Bi-based anode materials reported for NIBs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/large/nl9b04829_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b shows the first three cycles of galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles for Bi@Void@C-2 at the current density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The Bi@Void@C-2 electrode displays an initial discharge capacity of 684 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with a charge capacity of 316 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, revealing an initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 46%. The capacity loss of the first cycle is attributed to the decomposition of electrolyte to the formation of the SEI, irreversible side reaction between the carbon layer with the functional group and electrolyte, and little of the electrode material exfoliated from the current collector. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref26 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref26 ref34" aria-label="reference 15, 26 and 34">(15,26,34)</a> All the potential plateaus in the charge/discharge curves match with the redox peaks in the CV curves. The Bi@Void@C-1 and Bi@Void@C-3 deliver initial discharge/charge capacities of 394/613 and 234/613 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The difference capacity of Bi@Void@C-1 and Bi@Void@C-3 samples relies on the different Bi contents in the composites. The cycling stability of three samples at a current density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c. The capacity drops in the initial cycles are probably attributed to the SEI formation and the irreversible Na<sup>+</sup> storage in the defect and functional group of carbon. The Bi@Void@C-1 presents the highest initial capacity as compared to the other two. However, the capacity drops seriously to 146 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the 100th cycle, because the limited void volume cannot accommodate the volume expansion. The <i>ex situ</i> TEM image of Bi@Void@C-1 was carried out to confirm our speculation. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/suppl_file/nl9b04829_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>, the carbon shell is broken, and the Bi nanoparticles fall out. The Bi@Void@C-2 and Bi@Void@C-3 exhibit better cycling stability, delivering capacities of 253 and 194 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 500 cycles, respectively. The Coulombic efficiency of both Bi@Void@C-2 and Bi@Void@C-3 quickly reach to 100%. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/suppl_file/nl9b04829_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S6 and S7</a> reveal the TEM images of Bi@Void@C-2 and Bi@Void@C-3 after 100 cycles, respectively. Their yolk shell structure stays intact, confirming that the optimized void space could buffer the volume expansion during the repeated charge/discharge. To subtract the capacity contribution from the carbon shell, we prepared hollow carbon nanospheres (HCNs) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/suppl_file/nl9b04829_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>) by removing the Bi nanospheres. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/suppl_file/nl9b04829_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a> displays the galvanostatic charge/discharge curves for first three cycles and cycling stability at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> of HCN. The reversible capacity of HCN is only 128 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> and reduces to a negligible capacity when the current density is over 20 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/suppl_file/nl9b04829_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>). The rate performances of these three samples were investigated after an activation process until the capacities achieve stability. As demonstrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d, the Bi@Void@C-2 can deliver reversible capacities of 270, 265, 255, 246, 231, and 190 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at current densities of 1, 2, 5, 10, 20, and 50 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Even at a high rate of 100 A g<sup>–1</sup>, a high reversible capacity of 173 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> can still remain, meaning that the battery can achieve charge/discharge within about 6.2 s. When the current density returns to 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the electrode can still show a stable capacity of about 266 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. As a comparison, the Bi@Void@C-3 exhibits a better performance than Bi@Void@C-1 when the current density is lower than 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Afterward, the rate capacity of Bi@Void@C-1 is superior to that of Bi@Void@C-3 when the current density is higher than 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>. This phenomenon is due to the ion diffusion length of Bi@Void@C-3 being longer than that of Bi@Void@C-1. On the other hand, the discharge capacities of both Bi@Void@C-1 and Bi@Void@C-3 fade rapidly at the current density of 100 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p><p>To understand the reaction kinetics of sodium alloying with Bi of these samples, the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was carried out. The electrodes were cycled using a series of current densities of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> for 10 min and left on open circuit for 40 min to reach a quasithermodynamic equilibrium potential. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/suppl_file/nl9b04829_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>, the Bi@Void@C-1 delivers a smaller overpotential at the first voltage platform of 0.59 V as compared to Bi@Void@C-2 and Bi@Void@C-3. However, the Bi@Void@C-2 shows the minimum polarization voltage of these samples during the second voltage platform of 0.4 V, because the Bi@Void@C-1 with limited void space exhibits an increased overpotential during the alloying process, and Bi@Void@C-1 possesses a long diffusion path. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/suppl_file/nl9b04829_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a> reveals the Nyquist plots of the Bi@Void@C-2 electrode for the fresh and cycled state, which is composed of a depressed semicircle in the range of high to middle frequencies and an inclined line at low frequencies. The depressed semicircle and inclined line correspond to charge transfer impedance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) and Warburg resistance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> The fresh electrode exhibits <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of about 30 Ω and the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> gradually become smaller with the increase number of cycles. After 200 cycles, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> reduces below 1 Ω, revealing that the Bi@Void@C-2 can retain the high kinetics of the electrochemical reaction. Remarkably, the Bi@Void@C-2 possesses an excellent cycling stability at a high current density of 20 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Even after 10 000 cycles, this electrode still represents a capacity as high as 198 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, revealing a capacity retention of about 96% with respect to the 10th cycle (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e). The specific capacity decreases in the first several hundred cycles, which is due to the partial decomposition of the electrolyte, the formation and stabilization of the SEI film, and the irreversible reaction between sodium ions and the residual oxygen-containing functional group on the surface of carbon. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 36 and 37">(36,37)</a> After several hundred cycles, the capacity gradually increases which is attributed to the Bi nanoparticle refinement with improvement of sodium-ion accessibility on the Bi@Void@C-2 upon cycling. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 38 to 42">(38−42)</a> To the best of our knowledge, the capacity retention of the Bi@Void@C-2 at such a high rate is higher than that of other Bi-based anode materials for NIBs, including Bi@N–C, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> Bi@Graphite, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> Bi Bulk, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> Bi@3DGF, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> 1D Bi@N–C, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> np-Bi<sub>2</sub>Sb<sub>6</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> Bi<sub>1.11</sub>Sb<sub>0.89</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> Bi/rGO, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> Bi/Ni, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> Bi/C, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> and FLB <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f).</p><p>To better explain the superior sodium storage performance of Bi@Void@C-2, the redox pseudocapacitance-like contribution was evaluated. The CV curves of this electrode at different scan rates from 0.1 to 0.2, 0.3, 0.5, and 0.8 mV s<sup>–1</sup> are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a. All the CV curves have a uniform shape with small polarized voltage, indicating a fast reaction kinetics process. The function relation formula between the peak current (<i>i</i>, mA) and sweep rate (<i>v</i>, mV s<sup>–1</sup>) is depicted as follows: <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48 ref49" aria-label="reference 48 and 49">(48,49)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"></span></p><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/medium/nl9b04829_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i4"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq4"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/medium/nl9b04829_m004.gif" alt="" id="_i5"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(4)</span></div></div></span>where <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are two parameters. The <i>b</i> value of 0.5 means the electrochemistry diffusion-controlled process, whereas the value of 1 suggests a capacitive process. When the particles’ size reduces to nanoscale, the actual reaction process shows a state between the capacitive behavior and diffusion-controlled process. The <i>b</i> value of every redox peak can be obtained according to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="eq 4">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="4">4</a>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b shows that the <i>b</i> values of the four redox peaks are 0.59, 0.89, 0.70, and 0.80, respectively. The ratio of capacitive contribution can be work out according to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq5" aria-label="eq 5">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq5" aria-label="5">5</a>, as follows: <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50 ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51" aria-label="reference 50 and 51">(50,51)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq5"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/medium/nl9b04829_m005.gif" alt="" id="_i6"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(5)</span></div></div></span>The current at fixed potential is composed of a capacitive contribution (<i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i>) and diffusion-controlled contribution (<i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>0.5</sup>). As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c, the detailed surface-controlled contribution of Bi@Void@C-2 is 96.9% at a scan rate of 0.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. The percentages of two different charge storages at other sweep rates are summarized in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d, demonstrating an incremental pseudocapacitance ratio along with the increase of sweep rate from 0.1 to 0.8 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. Consequently, the rate performance of Bi@Void@C-2 is enhanced due to the high proportion of the surface charge storage contribution.<p></p><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/medium/nl9b04829_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/large/nl9b04829_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) CV curves at various scan rates. (b) Relationship between the peak currents and scan rated in logarithmic. (c) Capacitive contribution at scan rate of 0.5 mV. (d) Contribution ratio of the capacitive and diffusion-controlled capacity versus scan rate of Bi@Void@C-2.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/large/nl9b04829_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>The <i>in situ</i> XRD measurement was carried out to investigate the reaction mechanism of Bi@Void@C-2 during the second sodiation/desodiation process. The collected XRD patterns are displayed in a contour plot (left panel), line plot (center panel), and corresponding sodiation/desodiation profile (right panel), respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>). During the sodiation process, the peaks representing the (012), (104), and (110) planes of Bi are attenuated gradually at the voltage window 1.5–0.67 V. The peaks corresponding to the (001), (100), (101), and (110) planes of NaBi appear in 0.67 V. In the first voltage platform of 0.67–0.48 V, the peaks of Bi fade away, and the peaks of NaBi enhance gradually, revealing the alloying reaction between Bi and Na to form NaBi. Upon further sodiation of the second voltage window 0.48–0.01 V, the new peaks revealing the (101), (103), and (201) planes of Na<sub>3</sub>Bi evolve and are accompanied by the vanishing of peaks of NaBi. The intensity of all Na<sub>3</sub>Bi peaks reaches its maximum until discharge to 0.01 V, demonstrating that the NaBi has transformed into Na<sub>3</sub>Bi. In the charge process, the Na<sub>3</sub>Bi was decomposed in two steps, which forms NaBi and Bi in the voltage windows 0.01–0.61 and 0.61–1.5 V, respectively. Therefore, the voltage plateaus of charge/discharge profiles of 0.59/0.72 and 0.40/0.55 V are assigned to the two-phase reaction of Bi/NaBi and NaBi/Na<sub>3</sub>Bi, respectively.</p><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/medium/nl9b04829_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/large/nl9b04829_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. <i>In situ</i> XRD analysis of Bi@Void@C-2 upon the second galvanostatic sodiation/desodiation process. Contour plot (left panel), line plot (center panel), and corresponding sodiation/desodiation curve (right panel).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/large/nl9b04829_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>To further confirm the advantages of our rational design, the <i>in situ</i> TEM was carried out to observe the structural evolution during the live sodiation/desodiation process using a nanobattery. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52 ref53 ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52 ref53 ref54" aria-label="reference 52 to 54">(52−54)</a> As schematically shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a, the <i>in situ</i> electrochemical experiment setup contains Bi@Void@C-2 attached on a gold (Au) rod, and Na<sub>2</sub>O/Na coated on a tungsten (W) rod. The naturally grown Na<sub>2</sub>O on Na served as a solid electrolyte. When applying a negative bias (−1 V) on the Au end, the Bi@Void@C-2 is sodiated, and desodiation is realized when applying a positive bias (1 V). The time-resolved TEM images upon the sodiation/desodiation process are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b–h captured from <i>in situ</i> TEM video (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/suppl_file/nl9b04829_si_002.mp4" class="ext-link">Video S1</a>). During the sodiation process, the typical inner core of Bi nanoparticles of Bi@Void@C-2 (labeled by red/green arrows) underwent huge expansion with the diameter increasing from 160/131 to 224/187 nm, indicating a 274%/287% volume expansion, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b–f). Benefiting from the well-designed structure provided optimized void space to accommodate the volume change, no cracking, nor any notable change of the diameter of the carbon shell of Bi@Void@-2 is observed. After the full sodiation, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>g,h, the diameter of the Bi yolk shrank from 224/187 to 165/141 nm, respectively. The robust structural stability of Bi@Void@C-2 is further evaluated by <i>in situ</i> TEM in multiple sodiation/desodiation cycles by applying a constant bias (−3/3 V) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/suppl_file/nl9b04829_si_003.mp4" class="ext-link">Video S2</a>). The trending of volume change is similar to that of small bias (−1/1 V) for the first cycle (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>i–k). During the subsequent cycles, the Bi@Void@C-2 keeps its structural integrity well without any structural degradation (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>l–o), confirming the excellent stable structure. From all the observations above, we can clearly see that the Bi@Void@C-2 electrodes possess a superior structural stability in sodiation/desodiation cycles, which can well retain its structural integrity without observing any instable structural changes such as cracking and fractures, endowing the excellent cyclic stability.</p><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/medium/nl9b04829_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/large/nl9b04829_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a) Schematic illustration of the <i>in situ</i> TEM nanobattery setup. (b–h) Time-resolved TEM images of Bi@Void@C-2 during the sodiation/desodiation process. (i–o) <i>In situ</i> TEM images of Bi@Void@C-2 at the pristine state and after different sodiation/desodiation cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/large/nl9b04829_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>The DFT theoretical calculation and AIMD calculation are performed to figure out the sodiation kinetics at the atomic level. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> To obtain an estimate of barrier energy Na migration from one center of a dodecahedron to another center of a dodecahedron, we have calculated the two diffusion paths for Na movements along [100] and [110] directions (path 1 and path 2, respectively), exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>a,b and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>c,d, respectively. The energy barrier profiles are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>e; the activation energy barrier for Na diffusion along the path 1 and path 2 is 1.28 and 1.92 eV, respectively, demonstrating that the Na is more inclined to diffusion in Bi along the [001] direction. Moreover, the respective mean squared displacement (MSD) of Na and Bi atoms during the AIMD simulation is calculated based on the following formula: <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38 ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref56" aria-label="reference 38 and 56">(38,56)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"></span></p><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq6"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/medium/nl9b04829_m006.gif" alt="" id="_i7"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(6)</span></div></div>where <i>N</i> is the number of atoms, <i>x</i><sub><i>n</i></sub>(0) is the reference position of the Na and Bi atoms, and <i>x</i><sub><i>n</i></sub>(t) represents the reference position at <i>t</i> time. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>f, the diffusion rate of Na is obviously faster than that of Bi owing to the mass of Na being much smaller than Bi. The snapshots of the AIMD simulation at the original state, 480, 750, and 2000 fs are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>g. The Na inserts into the Bi crystal and is bonded with Bi atoms at a short time of 480 fs, revealing fast reaction dynamics. The MSD of Na atoms increases rapidly until the time reaches about 1300 fs, implying that the Na further enters the Bi crystal.<p></p><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/medium/nl9b04829_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/large/nl9b04829_0008.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. Sodium diffusion paths in bismuth: (a, b) path 1 and (c, d) path 2. (e) Corresponding energy barrier profiles. (f) MSD versus simulation time. (g) Snapshots of AIMD simulation. The red and blue balls represent Na and Bi atoms, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829/asset/images/large/nl9b04829_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b04829&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="last">In summary, Bi@Void@C yolk–shell structures have been rationally designed and successfully synthesized. When used as the anode for NIBs, the optimized yolk–shell structure with the suitable void space (Bi@Void@C-2) has been engineered to accommodate the volume expansion during repeat charge/discharge, as made evident by <i>in situ</i> TEM observations. The optimized sample of Bi@Void@C-2 shows an unprecedented rate performance of 173 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 100 A g<sup>–1</sup>, implying that it can be charged or discharge in 6.2 s. Besides, the cycling life of Bi@Void@C-2 at a high rate surpassed other Bi-based materials for NIBs reported in the literature, delivering a reversible capacity of 198 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 10 000 cycles at 20 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The excellent electrochemical performance of Bi@Void@C-2 can be attributed to the rational yolk–shell structure; the outer layer of the thin carbon shell is conductive to improving the electronic conductivity and avoiding the aggregation of the Bi nanoparticles, as elaborated by experimental investigations and theoretical calculations. Optimizing the void space to accommodate the volume expansion during alloying/dealloying could be extended to other alloy mechanism anode materials (i.e., Si, P, Sb) that undergo large volume changes during operation and shed light on developing advanced anode materials for high-performance NIBs as well as other rechargeable batteries.</p><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info"                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-ion batteries, recent advances and present challenges to become low cost energy storage systems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Palomares, Veronica; Serras, Paula; Villaluenga, Irune; Hueso, Karina B.; Carretero-Gonzalez, Javier; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5884-5901</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoRYtxfVVW_sLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhkMREAQ36dZg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XivFWnu7s%253D&amp;md5=72869139086b8ee9adec746418ff84b1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201802565"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ong, S.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries offer a potential alternative or complementary system to lithium-ion batteries, which are widely used in many applications.  For this purpose, layered O3-type NaCrO2 for use as a cathode material in sodium-ion batteries was synthesized via an emulsion-drying method.  The produced NaCrO2 was modified using pitch as a carbon source and the products were tested in half and full cells using a NaPF6-based non-aq. electrolyte soln.  The carbon-coated NaCrO2 cathode material exhibits excellent capacity retention with superior rate capability up to a rate of 150 C (99 mA h (g oxide)-1), which corresponds to full discharge during 27 s.  The surface conducting carbon layer plays a critically important role in the excellent performance of this cathode material.  We confirmed the reaction process with sodium using X-ray diffraction and X-ray absorption spectroscopy.  Thermal anal. using time-resolved X-ray diffraction also demonstrated the structural stability of carbon-coated Na0.5CrO2.  Due to the excellent performance of the cathode material described herein, this study has the potential to promote the accelerated development of sodium-ion batteries for a large no. of applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrdoV2EZhlfBrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhkMREAQ36dZg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXosVKgtLg%253D&amp;md5=4a8043bc0b2e1d073e5df56ab1b59e77</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201702024"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, X.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lou, X.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered metal oxides have attracted increasing attention as cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  However, the application of such cathode materials is still hindered by their poor rate capability and cycling stability.  Here, a facile self-templated strategy is developed to synthesize uniform P2-Na0.7CoO2 microspheres.  Due to the unique microsphere structure, the contact area of the active material with electrolyte is minimized.  As expected, the P2-Na0.7CoO2 microspheres exhibit enhanced electrochem. performance for Na storage in terms of high reversible capacity (125 mAh/g at 5 mA/g), superior rate capability and long cycle life (86% capacity retention over 300 cycles).                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nanoflake-constructed porous Na3V2(PO4)3/C hierarchical microspheres as a bicontinuous cathode for sodium-ion batteries applications</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cao, Xinxin; Pan, Anqiang; Yin, Bo; Fang, Guozhao; Wang, Yaping; Kong, Xiangzhong; Zhu, Ting; Zhou, Jiang; Cao, Guozhong; Liang, Shuquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">60</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">312-323</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NEANCA</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2211-2855</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted considerable attention for large-scale energy storage systems as a promising alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) due to the huge availability and low-cost.  Yet the development of SIBs has been hindered by the low reversibility, sluggish ion diffusion, as well as large vol. variations.  Herein, we report an efficient hydrothermal method for fabricating hierarchical porous Na3V2(PO4)3/C (NVP/C) microspheres assembled from interconnected nanoflakes.  The NVP nanocrystals are uniformly wrapped by N-doped carbon layer.  As a half-cell cathode, the NVP/C porous microspheres exhibit superior rate capability (99.3 mA h g-1 at 100 C) and excellent cyclic stability (79.1% capacity retention over 10,000 cycles at 20 C).  A full-cell configuration coupled with NVP/C cathode and SnS/C fibers anode exhibits an estd. practical energy d. of 223 W h kg-1.  The superior performance can be ascribed to the hierarchical porous micro/nano structure along with N-doped carbon encapsulation, which provide bicontinuous electron/ion pathways, large electrode-electrolyte contact area, as well as robust structural integrity.  This work provides a promising approach for boosting the electrochem. performance of battery materials via the integration of hierarchical structure and heteroatoms doped carbon coating.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrMgezPm5NUfLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhYgoW50N0dtA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXmtFGrsLY%253D&amp;md5=718d0a4fd277c4f447404bbf35ad6f6c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201702212"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheng, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, J.</span></span> <i>Adv.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Unusual Na+ Ion Intercalation/Deintercalation in Metal-Rich Cu1.8S for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Park, Hyunjung; Kwon, Jiseok; Choi, Heechae; Shin, Donghyeok; Song, Taeseup; Lou, Xiong Wen David</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A key issue with Na-ion batteries is the development of active materials with stable electrochem. reversibility through the understanding of their sodium storage mechanisms.  We report a sodium storage mechanism and properties of a new anode material, digenite Cu1.8S, based on its crystallog. study.  It is revealed that copper sulfides (CuxS) can have metal-rich formulas (x ≥ 1.6), due to the unique oxidn. state of +1 found in group 11 elements.  These phases enable the unit cell to consist of all strong Cu-S bonds and no direct S-S bonds, which are vulnerable to external stress/strain that could result in bond cleavage as well as decompn.  Because of its structural rigidness, the Cu1.8S shows an intercalation/deintercalation reaction mechanism even in a low potential window of 0.1-2.2 V vs. Na/Na+ without irreversible phase transformation, which most of the metal sulfides experience through a conversion reaction mechanism.  It uptakes, on av., 1.4 Na+ ions per unit cell (∼250 mAh g-1) and exhibits ∼100% retention over 1000 cycles at 2C in a tuned voltage range of 0.5-2.2 V through an overall solid soln. reaction with negligible phase sepn.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoIy7w8uVgYIbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhYgoW50N0dtA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXjvFGgs70%253D&amp;md5=398781e6a7b18867463fe0d7f9765a1e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1039/C7EE01100H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, X.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lou, X.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Structure-designed synthesis of FeS2@C yolk-shell nanoboxes as a high-performance anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Zhiming; Lu, Tianchi; Song, Taeseup; Yu, Xin-Yao; Lou, Xiong Wen; Paik, Ungyu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1576-1580</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Pyrite (FeS2) is an attractive anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) with a high theor. capacity of 894 mAh g-1.  However, its practical application is greatly hindered by the rapid capacity fading caused by the large vol. expansion upon sodiation.  Tuning the morphol. and structure at nanoscale and applying a higher cut-off voltage are essential to address this issue.  Here, a facile etching method coupled with a novel sulfidation-in-nanobox strategy is developed to synthesize unique FeS2@C yolk-shell nanoboxes.  The as-obtained FeS2@C nanoboxes reveal excellent sodium storage performance.  The remarkable electrochem. properties are attributed to the elaborate yolk-shell nanoarchitecture.  In particular, it delivers a high specific capacity of 511 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1 after 100 cycles.  Furthermore, a high specific capacity of 403 mAh g-1 even at 5 A g-1 is delivered.  Most impressively, a stable capacity of 330 mAh g-1 can still be retained at 2 A g-1 even after 800 cycles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrvJjmhFrYQyrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh6L0NYl-2WgA"> &gt;&gt;         ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1936-0851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Even though advocated as the potential low-cost alternatives to current lithium-ion technol., the practical viability of sodium-ion batteries remains illusive and depends on the development of high-performance electrode materials.  Very few candidates available at present can simultaneously meet the requirements on capacity, rate capability, and cycle life.  Herein, we report a high-temp. soln. method to prep. Ni3S4 nanorods with uniform sizes.  These colloidal nanorods readily self-assemble side by side and form microsized superstructures, which unfortunately negates the nanoscale feature of individual nanorods.  To this end, we further introduce two-dimensional graphene nanosheets as the spacer to interrupt nanorod self-assembly.  Resultant composite presents a marked advantage toward electrochem. storage of Na+ ions.  We demonstrate that in half-cells it exhibits large reversible specific capacity in excess of 600 mAh/g, high rate capability with &gt;300 mAh/g retained at 4 A/g, and great cycle life at different current rates.  This anode material can also be combined with the NASICON-type Na3V2(PO4)3 cathode in full cells to enable large capacity and good cyclability.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo24C8mtWl5SrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh6L0NYl-2WgA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXit1Wiu7Y%253D&amp;md5=e705922a40d1ca9d5991f7408ec4bbe6</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201801812"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qian, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lv, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Feng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, S.</span></span> <i>Adv.                         cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nanosized-bismuth-embedded 1D carbon nanofibers as high-performance anodes for lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yin, Hong; Li, Qingwei; Cao, Minglei; Zhang, Wei; Zhao, Han; Li, Chong; Huo, Kaifu; Zhu, Mingqiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Research</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
